<div id="review_3175170350" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3175170350" />
    <a title="Oleksandr Zholud" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4743119-oleksandr-zholud"><img alt="Oleksandr Zholud" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1506182069p2/4743119.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3175170350?book_show_action=true">Feb 07, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Oleksandr Zholud" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Oleksandr Zholud" href="/user/show/4743119-oleksandr-zholud">Oleksandr Zholud</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audible Audio " href="/book/show/30257522-spin">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3175170350" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1801341270473278466">This is a SF novel of ‘what if’ variety. It won Hugo in 2006. I read is as a part of monthly reading in February 2020 at <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/group/show/474371-hugo-nebula-awards-best-novels' rel='nofollow noopener'>Hugo & Nebula Awards: Best Novels</a> group.<br/><br/>A slight spoiler will follow in order to give an idea about the what if scenario.<br/><br/>The story starts with a trio of teenagers. Jason and Diane are twins, but they chose completely opposite paths: Jase is a scientist, groomed by his entrepreneur father to become a heir to the tech-empire; Diane is much more interested in spiritual and immate</span>
  <span id="freeText1801341270473278466" style="display:none">This is a SF novel of ‘what if’ variety. It won Hugo in 2006. I read is as a part of monthly reading in February 2020 at <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/group/show/474371-hugo-nebula-awards-best-novels" rel="nofollow noopener">Hugo &amp; Nebula Awards: Best Novels</a> group.<br /><br />A slight spoiler will follow in order to give an idea about the what if scenario.<br /><br />The story starts with a trio of teenagers. Jason and Diane are twins, but they chose completely opposite paths: Jase is a scientist, groomed by his entrepreneur father to become a heir to the tech-empire; Diane is much more interested in spiritual and immaterial, at least partially due to her father’s fixation solely on her brother. Tyler, the narrator, is a year younger friend of the twins, in love with Diane. While they are out in the night, the stars suddenly disappear. It happened throughout the globe – the planet is covered with some kind of a barrier, disengaging it from the rest of the universe. Sun is replaced by a shining perfect disc and tides are still present, but nothing can be seen (or any signal received) thru the barrier. A bit later there is a big reveal: the space rocket can pass the barrier in both directions to find out that <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[time goes significantly faster outside, roughly 100 mln year per one Earth year. Therefore, people can outlive our Sun, which was born 4.6 bn years ago and should expand to a red giant (with borders approx. on Earth orbit!) and then turn into a white dwarf in the next 5 bn years (or 50 new Earth years) <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />The rest of the story it an attempt to deal with what happened, by joining projects to understand the barrier (Jason), religious cults to meet the End (Diane) or just live their life (by going to get MD, Tyler). There main storyline takes the characters through their lives, while the additional ‘flashforward’ storyline follows Tyler as he evades the law.<br /><br />The book is the first volume of the trilogy but can be read as a standalone. There is a cliffhanger at the end but after all main questions were answered. <br /><br />It was a delight to read a SF novel that deals with a ‘what if’ scenario that doesn’t go to the current hot social topics of a lot of modern SF, but presents a challenge for readers: “what would I do in this case?”<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1801341270473278466" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_182588974" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/182588974" />
    <a title="Amy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/563794-amy"><img alt="Amy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1603051442p2/563794.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/182588974?book_show_action=true">Jul 06, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Amy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Amy" href="/user/show/563794-amy">Amy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/6581795-spin">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/563794-amy?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/563794-amy?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/563794-amy?shelf=favorite-author">favorite-author</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/563794-amy?shelf=2011-books-read">2011-books-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/563794-amy?shelf=kindle">kindle</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer182588974" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer703575414303236009">Why did the stars suddenly go out on an October night? <br/><br/>This is the type of book that you wish someone else is reading at the exact same time as you are so that you can immediately discuss all the "did that just happen?" moments. I really wish my husband had been reading it at the same time as me so that I could ask if he'd ever seen any of the various plot elements put together this way in his vast reading of sci-fi. It's a story about time travel, but it's not. It's a story about an apocalyptic</span>
  <span id="freeText703575414303236009" style="display:none">Why did the stars suddenly go out on an October night? <br /><br />This is the type of book that you wish someone else is reading at the exact same time as you are so that you can immediately discuss all the "did that just happen?" moments. I really wish my husband had been reading it at the same time as me so that I could ask if he'd ever seen any of the various plot elements put together this way in his vast reading of sci-fi. It's a story about time travel, but it's not. It's a story about an apocalyptic event, but it's not. It's a story about alien contact, but it's not. It's a story about meeting god, but it's not. And that's about all that I can say without giving away entirely too much. It's much better to go in blind. <br /><br />Long before I finished reading, this had become an instant favorite. It's one of those books you want to recommend to nearly everyone. Robert Charles Wilson writes in a way that is highly accessible to even non sci-fi readers. It took me reading nearly the entire book before I realized he's also the writer of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/5997978.Julian_Comstock_A_Story_of_22nd_Century_America" title="Julian Comstock A Story of 22nd-Century America by Robert Charles Wilson" rel="nofollow noopener">Julian Comstock: A Story of 22nd-Century America</a>, another book I fully enjoyed this year. Of course, I'm looking forward to reading the sequels to <i>Spin</i> (the 3rd in the series just came out), but I'm also determined to read everything else he's written. I'm smitten. Truly and absolutely smitten.<br /><br /><b>Edit After 2nd Reading</b><br />Even after a second read of this book, and it's still at the top of my list of all time favorites. But I think it's because the concept really appeals to me as one who loves time travel novels more for imagining the future than reliving the past. What is the possible future evolution of man taken to the extreme? The sequels go far beyond what I expected in showing this. Would we be more likely to take on big projects requiring thousands or millions of years to come to fruition if we were able to see the end results for ourselves with the help of a sort of time travel? How would humans react to a tangible and undeniable evidence of intelligent life beyond humanity? Would they assume it was the hand of a god or an alien intelligence? How would humanity react to an impending apocalypse? <br /><br />Reading this the second time in light of current world events, I'm seeing that the author hit the nail on the head with how a large portion of humanity would be in denial because they can't understand what's going on around them. They'd treat scientific explanations or predictions of the future as "fake news"--something political, a coverup, a scare tactic, anti-religious rhetoric, etc.--just as Jennifer mentioned that people do today concerning climate change, extinctions, etc. Even those who believed and understood the coming apocalypse would have different reactions: become depressed, party like there's no tomorrow, live like every day is just an ordinary day, seek solace in religion, or look for a solution. And I think Wilson has aptly shown these things.<br /><br />Ratings from my friends on this book seem to be either in the hate-it or love-it camp with not much in between. Those who like it seem to enjoy it as a novel exploring a big idea and people's reaction to it. Those who don't like it often don't like that it jumps back and forth in time. I prefer a straightforward novel, but it didn't bother me. <br /><br />Some people mention not believing in the characters, but they seemed like normal people to me: the gruff and impersonal father, the genius scientist brother married to his work, the sister who doesn't know how to express her feelings or know her own feelings because she's grown up in an environment where feelings are kept under wraps, and the neighbor boy whose personality is derived from always just being along for the ride because he's been drawn into the orbit of this family. In fact, I feel as if the characters have more depth in this novel than in MOST of Wilson's other novels (including the final novel of this series which is a little character-flat). <br /><br />As for the sequels, they follow a second set of characters and deal with terraforming, augmented human biology, nanobots, collective hive-mind networking, and star gates. Just the execution of the scale of the series and the concepts were impressive to me. Just how far can human evolution go? What's possible? What are the repercussions? If those concepts sound interesting to you and you enjoyed this novel, you'll be just as besotted with the rest of the series. </span>
  <a data-text-id="703575414303236009" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_98034094" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/98034094" />
    <a title="Trudi" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1416912-trudi"><img alt="Trudi" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1446613110p2/1416912.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/98034094?book_show_action=true">Apr 11, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Trudi" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Trudi" href="/user/show/1416912-trudi">Trudi</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1416912-trudi?shelf=survive-this">survive-this</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1416912-trudi?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1416912-trudi?shelf=2011">2011</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1416912-trudi?shelf=apocalyptic-types">apocalyptic-types</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer98034094" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2201304802999455746">The best way to come at this novel would be completely blind, not knowing a thing of what it’s about. My complaint about most movies these days is that too much is revealed in the trailers, so much so that the movie in its entirety is often a disappointment. For <i>Spin</i> to really work its magic on you the less you know the better. If you’re not expecting it, the awesome plot and the ramifications for the characters involved will hit you like a jack-hammer to the solar plexus. The good news is, if y</span>
  <span id="freeText2201304802999455746" style="display:none">The best way to come at this novel would be completely blind, not knowing a thing of what it’s about. My complaint about most movies these days is that too much is revealed in the trailers, so much so that the movie in its entirety is often a disappointment. For <i>Spin</i> to really work its magic on you the less you know the better. If you’re not expecting it, the awesome plot and the ramifications for the characters involved will hit you like a jack-hammer to the solar plexus. The good news is, if you read up on the book and know a fair amount before you begin, the intricate story and how it unfolds will still impress you, and engage you to the last page. <br /><br />Don’t let the science-fiction elements scare you away if that’s not your thing, because while at its core it is a very science driven story, it is not overly burdened with scientific jargon and explanations. The scenario is easy to grasp, seemingly plausible (ergo endlessly <i>frightening</i> and <i>exciting</i>). Wilson is a talented writer and his tale is well told, and he doesn’t sacrifice his characters to plot – the way some big budget movies will sacrifice story and characters to special effects. Ty, Diane and Jase are believable, likable, flawed characters, richly drawn. You live through the Spin with them and hold your breath wondering how it’s all going to end. Theirs is a story of friendship, and the bonds that bring us together as children, and keep us together as adults, even when the world is falling apart and the miles and years pile up around you. <br /><br />For fans of apocalyptic / dystopian books, this is a must read. It’s not only a human survival story, but bravely, with keen insight, explores rich philosophical terrain regarding Earth’s place in a larger unknowable Universe. Are we alone? And if we are not, who is keeping us company and to what purpose? There’s not much more I can say, without giving salient plot points away, and I don’t want to get anywhere close to doing that. Remember, the less you know the better. Take a chance and pick up this book as blind as you can –- I promise you won’t regret it. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="2201304802999455746" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1768450581" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1768450581" />
    <a title="ashley c" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/24679550-ashley-c"><img alt="ashley c" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1478225922p2/24679550.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1768450581?book_show_action=true">Sep 26, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="ashley c" class="user" itemprop="url" name="ashley c" href="/user/show/24679550-ashley-c">ashley c</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/24679550-ashley-c?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/24679550-ashley-c?shelf=sfbc-challenge">sfbc-challenge</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/24679550-ashley-c?shelf=award-winning">award-winning</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1768450581" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13621363635037802728">Spin for me was exactly the sort of thing I can curl up with on the couch on a quiet evening after dinner - it's a thoughtful and character-focused book balanced out with consistent spurts of action that keeps you hooked. The story, in it's core, is centered around the lives of three best friends from chilldhood to adulthood as they experience the Spin, a shield covering planet Earth, clearly put there by alien intelligence. These three people experience and react to the Spin in very different, </span>
  <span id="freeText13621363635037802728" style="display:none">Spin for me was exactly the sort of thing I can curl up with on the couch on a quiet evening after dinner - it's a thoughtful and character-focused book balanced out with consistent spurts of action that keeps you hooked. The story, in it's core, is centered around the lives of three best friends from chilldhood to adulthood as they experience the Spin, a shield covering planet Earth, clearly put there by alien intelligence. These three people experience and react to the Spin in very different, relatable ways - grandiose ambitions thrive, dysfunctional coping mechanisms manifest, and relationships change in uncontrollable ways for better or worse. It's beautiful the way Wilson captures what is quintessentially human - by showing us how the world and all its governments, religions, civilians, criminals, businessmen, and scientists react to the Spin and how different agendas clash. <br /><br /><blockquote>
  <i><br />"How much do you really need to know about the moon and the stars when your life consists of scrounging enough biomass to feed yourself and your family?"</i>
</blockquote><br /><br />I love how it is the people of the story that moves the premise; the plot doesn't charge ahead, leaving its characters behind. The plot is the backbone of the story and the characters are the brains and the flesh. In this sense the characters have the autonomy. They hold your hand and lead you through their lives as they find their place in the post-Spin world. It's a heartwarming feeling.<br /><br /><blockquote>
  <i>"Diane and Jason had been born minutes apart but were obviously fraternal rather than identical siblings; no one but their mother called them twins. Jason used to say they were the product of "dipolar sperm penetrating oppositely charged eggs." Diane, whose IQ was nearly as impressive as Jason's but who kept her vocabulary on a shorter leash, compared them to "different prisoners who escaped from the same cell."</i>
</blockquote><br /><br />I'm really excited to read the next two installments for this series.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13621363635037802728" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_41083058" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/41083058" />
    <a title="Lightreads" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1836077-lightreads"><img alt="Lightreads" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1230606449p2/1836077.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/41083058?book_show_action=true">Dec 28, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lightreads" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lightreads" href="/user/show/1836077-lightreads">Lightreads</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1836077-lightreads?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1836077-lightreads?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer41083058" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1771719138874778757">One day in our near future, the moon and all the stars disappear from the sky. All of them at once, all over the world. Decades later and far away, Tyler remembers that night and all the years after as he grew up part of the generation that knew the world was going to end within the next fifty years. And I really cannot describe the plot with any more exactitude, because saying anything else would spoil one of the hundred complex threads woven into this story, and that would be a damn shame.<br/><br/>I ca</span>
  <span id="freeText1771719138874778757" style="display:none">One day in our near future, the moon and all the stars disappear from the sky. All of them at once, all over the world. Decades later and far away, Tyler remembers that night and all the years after as he grew up part of the generation that knew the world was going to end within the next fifty years. And I really cannot describe the plot with any more exactitude, because saying anything else would spoil one of the hundred complex threads woven into this story, and that would be a damn shame.<br /><br />I can say that this is what is known as a “hard SF” novel, and as such it is powered on ideas. Extraordinary ideas which test the boundaries of the universe as we know it, from the jaw-droppingly smart to the ones I just didn't buy. But this is also a book rich in people, from the very personal convolutions of Tyler’s existence in and out of the orbit of twins Jason and Diane, to the deft, evocative way Wilson describes how human societies themselves work.<br /><br />And that’s what’s so brilliant about this book – it’s got range. It plays Tyler and Diane and Jason’s lives on the same stage, with the same life and sympathy with which it plays the rise and fall of civilizations, the making of worlds, the human spirit in extremity at its best in grace and cleverness, and its worst in hopelessness and fear. There’s so much packed into this just-right 370 pages, from a slow and subtle love story to penetrating insights on just how people and governments work in long, wrenching crisis, and then the hard slam. As the why and the how of the plot are slowly revealed through Tyler’s memories and the past races to meet the present, the nature of the story lets Wilson do things with scale, with everything from the passage of time on the cosmic scale to the scope of human understanding. It’s riveting, sweet, charismatic, difficult to read in places.<br /><br />Which is neglecting the prose, which is lucid and adroitly theatrical and just right.<br /><br />“Dreams, Diane once said, are metaphors gone feral.”<br /><br />“What we were transplanting was not biology but human history, and human history, Jase had said, burned like a fire compared to the slow rust of evolution.”<br /><br />“There’s a phrase Pastor Bob Cobble liked to use back at Jordan Tabernacle. ‘His heart cried out to God.’ . . . But you have to parse the sentence. ‘His heart cried out’ – I think that’s all of us, it’s universal. You, Simon, me, Jason. Even Carol. Even E.D. When people come to understand how big the universe is and how short a human life is, their hearts cry out. Sometimes it’s a cry of joy, I think that’s what it was for Jason, I think that’s what I didn’t understand about him. He had the gift of awe. But I think for most of us it’s a cry of terror. The terror of extinction, the terror of meaninglessness. Our hearts cry out. Maybe to God, or maybe just to break the silence.”<br /><br />And that last quote is actually a much better portrait of the book than I’m managing.<br /><br />Wow. Pieces of this one will be with me for a long time.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1771719138874778757" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2292883369" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2292883369" />
    <a title="Aerin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/61519-aerin"><img alt="Aerin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1520192022p2/61519.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2292883369?book_show_action=true">Feb 11, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Aerin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Aerin" href="/user/show/61519-aerin">Aerin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/61519-aerin?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/61519-aerin?shelf=apocalyptic">apocalyptic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/61519-aerin?shelf=time-travel">time-travel</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/61519-aerin?shelf=twisty-turny">twisty-turny</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2292883369" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3519137729967178681"><i>(Original review date: 6 August 2011)</i><br/><br/>This is pretty typical Wilson: Some bizarro science fiction event happens, and the characters are all like, "What the fuck," and it basically proceeds according to formula from there. More weird shit, more "what the fuck," lots of technobabble, repeat, repeat, escalating weirdness, and so on.<br/><br/>Don't get me wrong - I really enjoyed this book. This time, instead of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/116413.The_Chronoliths' rel='nofollow noopener'>a bunch of spooky obelisks appearing out of nowhere</a>, or <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/116417.Mysterium' rel='nofollow noopener'>an entire town just up and teleporting to a</a></span>
  <span id="freeText3519137729967178681" style="display:none"><i>(Original review date: 6 August 2011)</i><br /><br />This is pretty typical Wilson: Some bizarro science fiction event happens, and the characters are all like, "What the fuck," and it basically proceeds according to formula from there. More weird shit, more "what the fuck," lots of technobabble, repeat, repeat, escalating weirdness, and so on.<br /><br />Don't get me wrong - I really enjoyed this book. This time, instead of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/116413.The_Chronoliths" rel="nofollow noopener">a bunch of spooky obelisks appearing out of nowhere</a>, or <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/116417.Mysterium" rel="nofollow noopener">an entire town just up and teleporting to an alternate dimension one day</a>, or <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/116412.Blind_Lake" rel="nofollow noopener">a government research site that suddenly gets mysteriously locked down by robots (with bonus lobster aliens!)</a>, the bizarro science fiction event is that one night the stars disappear. Completely. They're just gone. And when the sun rises the next day, it's not the sun at all. It's a fake sun. "What the fuck?" says the entire world. And the plot goes through its motions from there.<br /><br /><br />I discovered Wilson back in the sixth or seventh grade, when I found a copy of the long-out-of-print-even-then <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/970648.Gypsies" rel="nofollow noopener">Gypsies</a> at the library and fell in looooooooove with it. It was the first interdimensional dystopian YA mystery I'd ever encountered, and I mean, you never forget your first time, right? I'm telling you, it BLEW MY JUNIOR HIGH MIND. I still get all mushy thinking back on that book, or really, my experience of discovering that book. It was such a, "running out of time at the library, guess I'll grab the first thing with an interesting cover-- OOOOOOOH SHIT THIS IS INCREDIBLE, WHAT IS IT AND WHERE CAN I FIND MORE????" moment of serendipity.<br /><br />After that, I read everything by Wilson that I could get my hands on, and they all disappointed me by comparison.<br /><br />I hadn't read anything new by Wilson since that phase, so I'd always associated him with pubescence and the kind of suspect taste I had in <s>everything</s> books back then, and it never occurred to me to keep up with what he'd been doing in the meantime.<br /><br />So it took everyone and their nerdy brother gushing about how amazing this book was to get me to pick it up. It is, I think, the best book of his I've read since <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/970648.Gypsies" rel="nofollow noopener">Gypsies</a> - the premise is the most interesting, the plot structure and the pacing of ever-more-awesome reveals is the most effective, and the characters are the most fleshed-out (though still not exactly compelling - characterization is <i>not</i> Wilson's strong point). Still, for a "hard" SF novel, it's got enough of a human hook to more than balance out the big ideas and sciencey jargon.<br /><br />So although this is just another iteration of What Wilson Does, it's the <i>best</i> example of how effective and awesome What Wilson Does can be, when he does it right. If you're only going to read one by him (and you really only <i>need</i> to read one, unless you <i>really really</i> enjoy this particular formula), you should go with this one.<br /><br />Or <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/970648.Gypsies" rel="nofollow noopener">Gypsies</a>.<br /><br />I fucking love <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/970648.Gypsies" rel="nofollow noopener">Gypsies</a>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3519137729967178681" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_434931931" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/434931931" />
    <a title="Francine" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/705590-francine"><img alt="Francine" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1296600423p2/705590.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/434931931?book_show_action=true">Oct 15, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Francine" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Francine" href="/user/show/705590-francine">Francine</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Joy Macpherson</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/705590-francine?shelf=kindle">kindle</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/705590-francine?shelf=postapocalyptic">postapocalyptic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/705590-francine?shelf=modern-lit">modern-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/705590-francine?shelf=speculative">speculative</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/705590-francine?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/705590-francine?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/705590-francine?shelf=dystopian-world">dystopian-world</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer434931931" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11643687852169474700"><br/>First off, my breakdown of the basics:<br/><br/><b>Narrative:</b> 5-stars. Highly intelligent, compelling, wonderful world-building. It's a novel of grand ideas yet somehow, it maintained a certain sense of intimacy. While this is, at heart, sci-fi, it deals with many things including science, religion, faith, love, loss (including loss of hope, loss of self, loss of faith), the deterioration of humanity and humanity's intrinsic need for survival, sometimes at all costs.<br/><br/><b>Writing:</b> 5 stars. Utterly beautiful prose,</span>
  <span id="freeText11643687852169474700" style="display:none"><br />First off, my breakdown of the basics:<br /><br /><b>Narrative:</b> 5-stars. Highly intelligent, compelling, wonderful world-building. It's a novel of grand ideas yet somehow, it maintained a certain sense of intimacy. While this is, at heart, sci-fi, it deals with many things including science, religion, faith, love, loss (including loss of hope, loss of self, loss of faith), the deterioration of humanity and humanity's intrinsic need for survival, sometimes at all costs.<br /><br /><b>Writing:</b> 5 stars. Utterly beautiful prose, very intelligent, unbelievable imagery (both sensory and emotionally). Robert Charles Wilson took the time to tell his story his way, even if it meandered here and there every now and again, and most importantly, he didn't pander to the lowest common denominator.<br /><br /><b>Characters:</b> 4 stars. Wonderfully rich character development, realistic journeys and character arcs, sympathetic characters that the reader can easily relate to; very nuanced protagonists and antagonists, including antiheroes. Even the characters I didn't necessarily like were not truly unlikable - what drove them to be who they were was as much a part of them that you could understand why they were drawn that way.<br /><br /><b>Science:</b> 4 stars. The science was actually quite sound. Mind you, this is still science fiction, so there is a lot of it that is speculative in nature. Having said that, what I liked about it was that it was accessible and true <i>enough</i>. There are a lot of novels out there that have grand ideas but fall short on the science (e.g., <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/12401556.The_Age_of_Miracles" title="The Age of Miracles by Karen Thompson Walker" rel="nofollow noopener">The Age of Miracles</a>, which was overall well-written and a good story, but the science wasn't rigorous enough -- there were times when I felt slightly cheated because the author either skirted around the science or posited theories that were just unbelievable <i>to me</i>). I'm not going to say I bought 100% of what Robert Charles Wilson wrote for his explanation of why or how the Spin Barrier was erected (and there are still some parts where I'm a bit fuzzy), but I did appreciate all the thought and research he did. The science in Spin was fairly solid, and that's really all I'm asking for in sci-fi.<br /><br /><b>Overall:</b> 4.5 stars, which, in Goodreads parlance equals 5 stars.<br /><br />My thoughts in general:<br /><br />Framework narratives can be tricky. There are some authors who frame a story and touch on the framework or secondary narrative only at the beginning and end of the story. What I liked about Spin was that the main (Tyler's story from childhood through the present) and secondary (the far future, which is 4x10^9 AD) narratives are equally important, and Wilson spends as much time exploring the past as the present/future. They're inextricably linked, and time, both from Tyler's perspective and as a result of the Spin barrier, flows very much like a mobius strip, clockwise and counterclockwise within a Euclidean space.<br /><br />While this is a sci-fi novel, I would hazard a guess that this is probably closer to 40% sci-fi and 60% a character study, with the focus being on the relationship and interrelations among Tyler, Jason and Diane. This isn't like most sci-fi novels where the focus is mostly on us vs. aliens, or us vs. tech-gone-bad, or us vs. us-gone-bad-due-to-technological-advancements. Spin is more like one of those sprawling literary novels with a smattering of fantastical sci-fi peppered in every so often, just so that we don't forget that it's actually sci-fi. The speculative parts definitely color the decisions and life trajectories of the various characters, and while you can't ignore it when Wilson's focusing on it, it always fades to the background the rest of the time. What's focused on is a very human drama, dealing with unrequited love, friendships, loneliness, family and everything in between.<br /><br />The main characters (Tyler Dupree, Jason and Diane Lawton) all stood for something: Jason was uncompromisingly a man of science: a child genius, he was created and molded by his father to be the man he eventually became. Jason knew how to play the game politically in order to fuel his single-minded obsession: funneling government and scientific resources into understanding the Spin, at any cost. Diane, Jason's twin sister, was equally as gifted and as intelligent, but unlike Jason, she was the ignored child. In a way, her parents' lack of concern for her propelled her into the tailspin she entered as a teen. Shunning science, she absorbed everything that was anathema to Jason and her father: new age beliefs, twisted fundamentalist Christianity, a new reading on biblical apocalyptic prophecies. As much as Jason loved the Spin, Diane hated it and was almost uncompromising in her beliefs to refute the meaning of the Spin. What's interesting is that while she wholeheartedly took on a cowl of religious fervor, there was always a part of her that instinctively knew religion wasn't the answer but that she was willing to hold on to it because it was the only thing that <i>made sense</i> to her after the Spin.<br /><br />And then there's Tyler. Tyler was the twins' best friend from childhood, and the one constant in both Jason's and Diane's lives. Tyler stood for everything the twins never had: love, faith, loyalty, constancy. He was the poor kid looking in on the Big House (Tyler was the son of the twins' father's partner and friend; when his dad passed away, Tyler and his mother ended up living in a little cottage on the Lawtons' property. His mom became the Lawtons' housekeeper). He was the one who fell in love with Diane at age ten and who was enamored by Jason's intelligence. Growing up, the twins included Tyler in everything and he soaked up all that they offered -- lessons, toys, endless summer days, friendship, secrets. But in the same token, Tyler was the one who wanted and needed to get away from the Lawtons and the Big House. But in leaving the Lawtons behind, he became a shell, moving through life as if something were missing. Sure, he was successful; he became a doctor, had relationships, had a life. His later lovers inevitably always pointed out that Tyler was just coasting, was largely indifferent, that everything always came back to the Lawtons and that he couldn't give them up because he didn't want to. <br /><br />But I think he wouldn't give them up because they were as integral to him as he was to them. Both Jason and Diane relied on Tyler for various kinds of support. Tyler was Jason's lifeline to the outside world - sure, he shared things with Tyler that would have gotten both of them thrown into prison - but more than that, Tyler was Jason's link to humanity. Jason was too logical, too scientific, out of touch with the world and with people, but with Tyler, Jason was able to go back to a simpler time and just be Jason. Diane held on to Tyler because he provided her with whatever her religion, her husband and her family couldn't give her: namely unconditional, uncompromising love. Tyler almost functioned as the twins' soul. Similarly, both Jason and Diane was Tyler's brain and heart, respectively, and he couldn't function without having them in his life either. Whenever Tyler cut himself off from them, his life was empty, as empty as the Earth seemed once the Spin barrier occluded it from the galaxy and the universe. It was a very weird -- and some would say unhealthy -- symbiotic relationship the three of them shared. And despite their imperfect and utterly trying relationship, Tyler loved both of them.<br /><br />One of my favorite parts of the book explains their convoluted relationship (in this excerpt, Tyler is being tended to by Ibu Ina, a Minang physician in the future): <br /><br /> <blockquote>Tyler said "Not half as beautifully as Jason did. It was like he was in love with the world, or at least the patterns in it. The music in it."<br /><br />"And Diane was in love with Jason?"<br /><br />"In love with being his sister. Proud of him."<br /><br />"And were you in love with being his friend?"<br /><br />"I suppose I was."<br /><br />"And in love with Diane."<br /><br />"Yes."<br /><br />"And she with you."<br /><br />"Maybe. I hoped so."<br /><br />"Then, if I may ask, what went wrong?"<br /><br />"What makes you think anything went wrong?"<br /><br />"You're obviously still in love. The two of you, I mean. But not like a man and a woman who have been together for many years. Something must have kept you apart. Excuse me, this is terribly impertinent."<br /><br />Yes, something had kept us apart. Many things. Most obviously, I supposed, it was the Spin. She had been especially, particularly frightened by it, for reasons I had never completely understood; as if the Spin were a challenge and a rebuke to everything that made her feel safe. What made feel safe? The orderly progression of life; friends, family, work -- a kind of fundamental <i>sensibility</i> of things, which in E.D. and Carol Lawton's Big House must already have seemed fragile, more wished-for than real.<br /><br />The Big House had betrayed her, and eventually even Jason had betrayed her: the scientific ideas he presented to her like peculiar gifts, which had once seemed reassuring -- the cozy major chords of Newton and Euclid -- became stranger and more alienating...a universe not only expanding but accelerating towards its own decay.<br /><br />...The Spin, when it came, must have seemed like a monstrous vindication of Jason's worldview--more so because of his obsession with it.... It was immensely powerful, terrifyingly patient, and blankly indifferent to the terror it had inflicted on the world. Imagining Hypotheticals, one might picture hyperintelligent robots or inscrutable energy beings; but never the touch of a hand, a kiss, a warm bed, or a consoling word.<br /><br />So she hated the Spin in a deeply personal way, and I think it was that hatred that ultimately led her to Simon Townsend and the NK movement. In NK theology, the Spin became a sacred event but also a subordinate one: large but not as large as the God of Abraham; shocking but less shocking than a crucified Savior, an empty tomb.</blockquote><br /><br />I was very pleasantly surprised to find out that this is only the first book in a trilogy. I already downloaded the second and third books. While the subsequent books won't have the Lawtons and Tyler in it, I'm still looking forward to seeing where Robert Charles Wilson will take me. I definitely think he's become one of my favorite authors now.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11643687852169474700" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('3jgmutVosakd+OmT05y2ZHNSfPje/gpx08cUKGZRIcEqt9cmDn8Y7+VhweYrApGbaMWySojscvQM/gQhbMS98g==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {

  }

Now, as he regains his faculties through a cure developed during the years of his near-fatal decline, he discovers that the world has changed and so has his place in it. He was a world-renowned poet. Now he is seventy-five years old, though by a medical miracle he looks much younger, and he’s starting over, for the first time unsure of his poetic gifts. Living with his son’s family, he has no choice but to learn how to cope with a new information age in which the virtual and the real are a seamless continuum, layers of reality built on digital views seen by a single person or millions, depending on your choice. But the consensus reality of the digital world is available only if, like his thirteen-year-old granddaughter Miri, you know how to wear your wireless access—through nodes designed into smart clothes—and to see the digital context—through smart contact lenses. \n \nWith knowledge comes risk. When Robert begins to re-train at Fairmont High, learning with other older people what is second nature to Miri and other teens at school, he unwittingly becomes part of a wide-ranging conspiracy to use technology as a tool for world domination. \n \nIn a world where every computer chip has Homeland Security built-in, this conspiracy is something that baffles even the most sophisticated security analysts, including Robert’s son and daughter-in law, two top people in the U.S. military. And even Miri, in her attempts to protect her grandfather, may be entangled in the plot. \n \nAs Robert becomes more deeply involved in conspiracy, he is shocked to learn of a radical change planned for the UCSD Geisel Library; all the books there, and worldwide, would cease to physically exist.       <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/47562-spin">Quotes from Spin</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;There are so many kinds of time. The time by which we measure our lives. Months and years. Or the <i>big</i> time, the time that raises mountains and makes stars. Or all the things that happen between one heartbeat and the next. Its hard to live in all those kinds of times. Easy to <i>forget</i> that you live in all of them.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/125928-there-are-so-many-kinds-of-time-the-time-by">63 likes</a>
            </nobr>
